Many organic ligands are known to be able to be used in neptunium coordination complexes: they include <a href="/wiki/Acetate" title="Acetate">acetate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propionate" title="Propionate">propionate</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glycolate" title="Glycolate">glycolate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxalate" title="Oxalate">oxalate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malonate" title="Malonate">malonate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phthalate" title="Phthalate">phthalate</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mellitate" title="Mellitate">mellitate</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Citrate" title="Citrate">citrate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yoshida77182_137-2"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida77182-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>Analogously to its neighbours, uranium and plutonium, the order of the neptunium ions in terms of complex formation ability is Np<sup>4+</sup> &gt; <span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> ≥ Np<sup>3+</sup> &gt; <span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>. (The relative order of the middle two neptunium ions depends on the <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligands</a> and solvents used.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yoshida77182_137-3"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida77182-137">[137]</a></sup> The stability sequence for Np(IV), Np(V), and Np(VI) complexes with monovalent inorganic ligands is <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">F<sup>−</sup></a> &gt; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dihydrogen_phosphate" title="Dihydrogen phosphate"><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>PO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span></a> &gt; <a href="/wiki/Thiocyanate" title="Thiocyanate">SCN<sup>−</sup></a> &gt; <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate"><span class="chemf nowrap">NO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span></a> &gt; <a href="/wiki/Chloride" title="Chloride">Cl<sup>−</sup></a> &gt; <a href="/wiki/Perchlorate" title="Perchlorate"><span class="chemf nowrap">ClO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span></a>; the order for divalent inorganic ligands is <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span></a> &gt; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_phosphate" title="Hydrogen phosphate"><span class="chemf nowrap">HPO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span></a> &gt; <a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate"><span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span></a>. These follow the strengths of the corresponding <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a>. The divalent ligands are more strongly complexing than the monovalent ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yoshida77182_137-4"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida77182-137">[137]</a></sup> <span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> can also form the complex ions [<span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>M<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>] (M = <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">Al</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gallium" title="Gallium">Ga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scandium" title="Scandium">Sc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indium" title="Indium">In</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">Fe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">Cr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhodium" title="Rhodium">Rh</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Perchloric_acid" title="Perchloric acid">perchloric acid</a> solution: the strength of interaction between the two cations follows the order Fe &gt; In &gt; Sc &gt; Ga &gt; Al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yoshida77182_137-5"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida77182-137">[137]</a></sup> The neptunyl and uranyl ions can also form a complex together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yoshida77182_137-6"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida77182-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Precursor_in_plutonium_production">Precursor in plutonium production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Precursor in plutonium production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An important of use of <sup>237</sup>Np is as a precursor in plutonium production, where it is irradiated with neutrons to create <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-238" title="Plutonium-238"><sup>238</sup>Pu</a>, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha_emitter" title="Alpha emitter">alpha emitter</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermal_generator" title="Radioisotope thermal generator">radioisotope thermal generators</a> for spacecraft and military applications.  <sup>237</sup>Np will capture a neutron to form <sup>238</sup>Np and <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> with a half-life of just over two days to <sup>238</sup>Pu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {^{237}_{93}Np + ^{1}_{0}n -&gt; ^{238}_{93}Np -&gt;[\beta^-][2.117 \ {\ce {d}}] ^{238}_{94}Pu}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/18a4a99dfdd1831d6e5ba84881bc0c24d9985cc8" style="vertical-align: -1.828ex; margin-top: -0.415ex; margin-bottom: -0.51ex; width:34.946ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p><sup>238</sup>Pu also exists in sizable quantities in <a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel" title="Spent nuclear fuel">spent nuclear fuel</a> but would have to be separated from other <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_plutonium" title="Isotopes of plutonium">isotopes of plutonium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yoshida703_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida703-139">[139]</a></sup> Irradiating neptunium-237 with electron beams, provoking <a href="/wiki/Bremsstrahlung" title="Bremsstrahlung">bremsstrahlung</a>, also produces quite pure samples of the isotope <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plutonium-236" title="Plutonium-236">plutonium-236</a>, useful as a tracer to determine plutonium concentration in the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yoshida703_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida703-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Weapons">Weapons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Weapons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Neptunium is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fissionable" title="Fissionable">fissionable</a>, and could theoretically be used as fuel in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fast_neutron_reactor" title="Fast neutron reactor">fast neutron reactor</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapon</a>, with a <a href="/wiki/Critical_mass" title="Critical mass">critical mass</a> of around 60 kilograms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rsc_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-rsc-71">[71]</a></sup> In 1992, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Energy" title="U.S. Department of Energy">U.S. Department of Energy</a> declassified the statement that neptunium-237 "can be used for a nuclear explosive device".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RDD-7_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-RDD-7-140">[140]</a></sup> It is not believed that an actual weapon has ever been constructed using neptunium. As of 2009, the world production of neptunium-237 by commercial power reactors was over 1000 critical masses a year, but to extract the isotope from irradiated fuel elements would be a major industrial undertaking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nevada_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nevada-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2002, researchers at the <a href="/wiki/Los_Alamos_National_Laboratory" title="Los Alamos National Laboratory">Los Alamos National Laboratory</a> briefly created the first known nuclear <a href="/wiki/Critical_mass" title="Critical mass">critical mass</a> using neptunium in combination with shells of <a href="/wiki/Enriched_uranium" title="Enriched uranium">enriched uranium</a> (<a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">uranium-235</a>), discovering that the critical mass of a bare sphere of neptunium-237 "ranges from kilogram weights in the high fifties to low sixties,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-criticality_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-criticality-1">[1]</a></sup> showing that it "is about as good a bomb material as [uranium-235]."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weiss_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-Weiss-27">[27]</a></sup> The United States Federal government made plans in March 2004 to move America's supply of separated neptunium to a nuclear-waste disposal site in <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nevada_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nevada-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physics">Physics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Physics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><sup>237</sup>Np is used in devices for detecting high-energy (MeV) neutrons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_in_nuclear_waste">Role in nuclear waste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Role in nuclear waste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Neptunium accumulates in commercial household ionization-chamber <a href="/wiki/Smoke_detector" title="Smoke detector">smoke detectors</a> from decay of the (typically) 0.2 <a href="/wiki/Microgram" title="Microgram">microgram</a> of americium-241 initially present as a source of <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">ionizing radiation</a>. With a half-life of 432 years, the americium-241 in an <a href="/wiki/Smoke_detector#Design" title="Smoke detector">ionization smoke detector</a> includes about 3% neptunium after 20 years, and about 15% after 100 years.
</p><p>Neptunium-237 is the most mobile <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinide</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Deep_geological_repository" title="Deep geological repository">deep geological repository</a> environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> This makes it and its predecessors such as <a href="/wiki/Americium-241" title="Americium-241">americium-241</a> candidates of interest for destruction by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">nuclear transmutation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> Due to its long half-life, neptunium will become the major contributor of the total <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiotoxicity" title="Radiotoxicity">radiotoxicity</a> in 10,000 years. As it is unclear what happens to the containment in that long time span, an extraction of the neptunium would minimize the contamination of the environment if the nuclear waste could be mobilized after several thousand years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nevada_141-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nevada-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_role_and_precautions">Biological role and precautions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Biological role and precautions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Neptunium does not have a biological role, as it has a short half-life and occurs only in small traces naturally. Animal tests showed that it is not absorbed via the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digestive_tract" title="Digestive tract">digestive tract</a>. When injected it concentrates in the bones, from which it is slowly released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley345347_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-emsley345347-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Finely divided neptunium metal presents a fire hazard because neptunium is <a href="/wiki/Pyrophoricity" title="Pyrophoricity">pyrophoric</a>; small grains will ignite spontaneously in air at room temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yoshida724_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida724-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
